==PAGE:6==
==PAGE:7==
Abstract
In this project a BIGCC power plant with 5 MW power output was modeled with the simulation tool
DNA based on a concept developed by Babcock & Wilcox Vølund. The power plant only produces power
and is fueled by wood chips that are gasiﬁed in an updraft gasiﬁer. The tars are separated from the product
gas and the dry and clean gas is burned in gas engines. The tar is burned in an adiabatic furnace which
produces hot ﬂue gas utilized for steam production in a boiler to be passed through a steam turbine in a
steam cycle.
The main purpose of the work was to investigate whether the integration of an ORC unit would be a
feasible choice. A thermodynamic analysis followed by a thermoeconomic analysis were performed to
achieve this goal. The plant model revealed that the heat sources of the plant could be used more effectively
within the plant. Design modiﬁcations were made, that did not include an ORC unit, which resulted in
increased efﬁciency and a lower levelized cost of electricity. These design changes involved lowering the
pressure of the largest heat need of the plant, which resulted in a better use of low temperature heat sources
and increased heat recovery in the steam cycle.
The integration of an ORC unit with the power plant did increase the efﬁciency somewhat but did not
turn out to be cost-effective due to low evaporating temperatures in the proposed conﬁgurations. A better
result was achieved by modifying some of the design parameters of the power plant to achieve an increased
heat utilization in the steam cycle or an improved efﬁciency of the steam cycle. By these means the efﬁciency
of the power plant was raised from 35.68% to 39.15% and the levelized cost of electricity lowered from
0.3452 e/kWh to 0.3247 e/kWh.

Preface
This project is a 32.5 ECTS Master’s Thesis handed in as a partial fulﬁllment for an MSc degree in
Engineering Design and Applied Mechanics at the Technical University of Denmark. The project work was
carried out in the period of May - October 2015.
This work has been hard at times but educating and quite enjoyable. I want to thank my supervisors
for their invaluable support over the last months. Lasse Røngaard Clausen mainly for helping me with the
DNA model, Leonardo Pierobon for good discussions about ORCs, Fredrik Haglind, my main supervisor,
for keeping everything together and Thomas Norman from Vølund for providing data on the power plant. I
would also like to thank Jan Bach Pedersen from Vølund for good answers to my questions regarding the
power plant.
I am thankful to my parents for being good role models and supporting me. My younger brother and
sister for being awesome and looking up to me and making me feel good about myself. My son, Þorbergur,
for being such a great guy and my daughter, Katla, for the joy she brings.
Last but not least I want to thank my lovely wife for keeping it real, most of the time, through all of this
and for supporting me even when I am caught up in extremely deep thoughts and cannot here a word she is
saying. Without her my home would be a wreck and my kids would be criminals and I would be a dirty poet
in a bar somewhere.
Bjarni Steinar Gunnarsson (s131636)

==PAGE:25==
CHAPTER 1
Introduction
Biomass is a renewable energy resource and is considered to be CO2 neutral. In the member states of the
European Union the share of biomass in primary energy production was 15.6% and 4.5% in electricity pro-
duction in 2013 (Eurostat (2015)). The most common energy conversion method for biomass is combustion
but thermal gasiﬁcation has also proved to be a ﬂexible and efﬁcient method of utilizing this energy resource
(Ahrenfeldt et al. (2013)).
Babcock & Wilcox Vølund has been on the forefront in developing an updraft gasiﬁcation technology
since 1988. As a result of this work a district heating gasiﬁcation plant was put into commercial use in
1993 in the Danish small town Harboøre. The plant uses wood chips as fuel and was later upgraded to a
CHP plant where gas engines were employed to burn the clean and dry product gas for power production.
The CHP plant has been operated successfully and with a thermal input of 5.2 MW it is running with an
electrical efﬁciency of 28% and a thermal efﬁciency of 55% (Ahrenfeldt et al. (2013)). Other plants using
this technology are now in operation in Japan as well.
The biomass integrated gasiﬁcation combined cycle (BIGCC) is a natural extension of the Harboøre
plant. The tar from the gasiﬁer is separated from the product gas and burned in an adiabatic furnace using
the ﬂue gas from the engines as an oxidizer. The hot ﬂue gas is used to produce steam in a boiler to drive a
steam turbine. This addition improves the electrical efﬁciency of the plant substantially.
The BIGCC can be designed as a CHP plant but it can also be designed as a power plant only. In this
project it is assumed that no heat is demanded from the plant and the only valuable output is electrical power.
When running in this mode it is important to utilize the waste heat for the internal heat needs of the plant
and to recover as much heat in the steam cycle as possible. If there still exists a non-negligible amount of
waste heat even though heat needs are covered and the steam cycle is optimized the integration of a low
temperature power cycle such as an organic Rankine cycle (ORC) might be used to improve the efﬁciency if
a cost-effective design can be achieved.
The main objectives of this study can be summarized as follows:
• Make a thermodynamic model of the BIGCC with 5 MW net power output according to data provided
by Vølund.
• Analyze the results of the model and come up with design modiﬁcations that can lead to better use of
waste heat within the power plant.
• Investigate the possibilities of integrating an ORC with the power plant.
• Perform a thermoeconomic analysis and compare the levelized cost of electricity (LCOE) for the
different design cases.
1
==PAGE:26==
2
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
• Reveal if integrating an ORC is a feasible option and compare that option with other suggestions.
The power plant is modeled in DNA (Dynamic network analysis) which is a tool for energy systems
analysis, developed by the Department of Mechanical Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark.
Heat exchanger networks (HENs) are designed for the design cases using pinch analysis. The cost balances
in the the thermoeconomic analysis are formulated and solved in the environment of EES (Engineering
equation solver).
The structure of the thesis can be outlined as:
• In chapter 2 the concept of the BIGCC power plant is explained with special emphasis on the updraft
gasiﬁer.
• In chapter 3 the DNA model is explained and the results given for a basic plant design based on target
data from Vølund.
• In chapter 4 the power plant is scaled up to have a net power output of 5 MW. The results are analyzed
and possibilities of improvement are revealed. The basics of pinch analysis are also given in this
chapter.
• In chapter 5, plant improvements are suggested, modeled and analyzed. These improvements aim at
utilizing the waste heat more effectively within the power plant. Three conﬁgurations of an ORC,
integrated with the plant, are also modeled and analyzed. The ORC technology is explained in this
chapter.
• In chapter 6 the thermoeconomic analysis is performed and the thermoeconomic theory is given.
• In chapter 7, thermoeconomic and thermodynamic results are compared and evaluated.
• In chapter 8 the conclusions from the work are stated.
==PAGE:27==
CHAPTER 2
The BIGCC power plant
In this chapter the BIGCC power plant concept is outlined with special emphasis on the updraft gasiﬁer.
The relation of the BIGCC to the CHP plant in Harboøre is also explained.
2.1
CHP plant in Harboøre
In 1993 Babcock & Wilcox Vølund put their ﬁrst gasiﬁcation plant into commercial use. It was a district
heating plant located in the small town Harboøre on the northwest coast of Jutland, Denmark. Wood chips
were chosen as a fuel for the plant since it is a convenient biomass type to work with. The air-blown updraft
gasiﬁer was developed by Vølund and in the ﬁrst years the gas was led to a boiler and burned. The plant was
modiﬁed in 2001 to produce power as well. The systems that were added to enable the conversion to a CHP
plant were:
• Gas cleaning plant.
• Electrostatic precipitator.
• Two motors with generators, 650 and 750 kW.
• 800 m3 heat accumulator tank.
• Tar and water cleaning system called TarWatC.
Currently, the CHP plant has a thermal output of 3400 kW and provides heat to approximately 560 heat
consumers and also to the municipal buildings of the town (Vølund (2011)). The thermal input to the gasiﬁer
is 5.2 MW and the electrical efﬁciency is 28% (Ahrenfeldt et al. (2013)). The layout of the plant is given in
ﬁgure 2.1.
The biomass integrated gasiﬁcation combined cycle (BIGCC) plant is a new concept plant developed by
Vølund based on the CHP plant in Harboøre. The same gasiﬁcation technology is used and the gas cleaning
plant and gas engines are also a part of the concept. The tarwater is treated differently and a steam cycle is
added to improve the electrical efﬁciency.
For the analysis of the BIGCC plant some valuable data from the Harboøre plant is used.
3
==PAGE:28==
4
CHAPTER 2. THE BIGCC POWER PLANT
Figure 2.1: Layout of the CHP gasiﬁcation plant in Harboøre. The ﬁgure is from Vølund (2011).
2.2
BIGCC
The BIGCC was initially developed for south Europe and the intension is to build the plant in southern
Italy. For this reason, throughout the project, economic and environmental conditions are deﬁned as if the
plant was located in Italy.
The layout of the BIGCC is given in ﬁgure 2.2. The plant can be designed as a CHP plant or as a power
plant. In this project it is assumed that there is only demand for power and therefore no heat production for
district heating is assumed to take place.
In the following sections the subsystems of the power plant are described with reference to ﬁgure 2.2.
Some components or modules of the power plant are not shown in the ﬁgure and do not enter the analysis at
any point. The main modules of this kind are:
• Fuel handling module. Handles the fuel on site and feeds it to the gasiﬁer.
• Chimney module. The ﬂue gas is emitted to the atmosphere through a steel chimney.
• Make-up water module. Water polishing plant is required to ensure the quality of water entering the
gasiﬁer and in the steam cycle.
==PAGE:29==
2.2. BIGCC
5
Figure 2.2: Overall layout of the plant.
==PAGE:30==
6
CHAPTER 2. THE BIGCC POWER PLANT
2.2.1
Gasiﬁer module
In the BIGCC an air-blown updraft gasiﬁer is used. Wood chips are used as fuel and the gasifying media
is steam and air. The wood chips should be derived from fresh and recently felled logs of wood. The plant
should primarily use wood chips from leaf-bearing trees (hardwood) but the use of wood chips from conifer
wood (softwood) can also be used. Additional requirements for the wood chips properties are given in table
2.1. Furthermore, the wood chips should be rounded and homogeneous and should match a certain size
distribution.
Table 2.1: Property requirements of the wood chips.
Value
Unit
Ash content
0-2
%
Ash softening temperature
>1000
◦C
Density
200-350
kg/m3
Moisture content
35-50
%
Lower heating value
8.4-11.6
MJ/kg
A sketch of the updraft gasiﬁer is shown in ﬁgure 2.3a. The fuel is fed from above and distributed evenly
with a rotating fuel distributor. The steam and air, which serve as gasifying media, is preheated and fed into
the gasiﬁer through a rotary grate at the bottom. The rotary grate distributes the gasifying media and extracts
the ash. It rotates only around one rotation per day. The product gases leave the gasiﬁer from the top.
(a) Updraft gasiﬁer.
(b) Gasiﬁcation process.
Figure 2.3: Updraft gasiﬁer and a basic model for the chemical reactions in an updraft gasiﬁer. Figure (a) is
adapted from Vølund (2011) and ﬁgure (b) is from Jørgensen and Heeb (2008).
==PAGE:31==
2.2. BIGCC
7
The gasiﬁer has four reaction zones shown in ﬁgure 2.3a and explained in ﬁgure 2.3b. With reference to
the ﬁgures the gasiﬁcation process can be explained as follows:
• Drying. In the top layer of the gasiﬁer the moisture in the biomass is evaporated by means of the hot
upward gas ﬂow. The high moisture content in this zone has the effect that the surface of the wood
chips acts as a ﬁlter and almost no ash leaves the gasiﬁer with the product gas.
• Pyrolysis. Further down the temperature increases to 150-800◦C in the pyrolysis zone. The wood
is heated without addition of oxidizing gases and volatiles and tar are released. Tar is a thick, black,
viscous liquid that is usually undesirable in gasiﬁers. In updraft gasiﬁers more tar is produced than
with any other gasiﬁcation technology. This is because the tars never move through a high temperature
zone where they might crack.
The tar contains 10-20% of the energy of the gas. It is divided into light tar and heavy tar. The
light tar is water solvable and has a HHV of around 12 MJ/kg. The heavy tar is made up from long
hydrocarbons and is not solvable in water. It is heavier than water and has a HHV of around 30 MJ/kg.
(Jørgensen and Heeb (2008)).
• Gasiﬁcation (reduction). In the pyrolysis zone the wood turns into charcoal (coke) and sinks to
the gasiﬁcation zone where the temperature is 800-1500◦C. In this zone the gasiﬁcation reactions
take place. The charcoal reacts with carbon dioxide, from the combustion zone, and steam, from the
gasifying media, to form carbon monoxide and hydrogen.
• Combustion (oxidation). In this zone the temperature is sufﬁciently high for combustion to take
place (1100-1300◦C). A large part of the charcoal in this zone burns in the oxygen derived from the
gasifying medium to form carbon dioxide.
The product gas leaves the gasiﬁer at 75◦C, water-saturated and containing a considerable amount of tar,
as previously mentioned. In ﬁgure 2.4 an example of the composition and ﬂow of the dry and clean product
gas is shown. The data is retrieved from measurements over the course of four hours on the Harboøre gasifer.
Both composition and ﬂow are subject to rather large ﬂuctuations.
Figure 2.4: Product gas composition measurements from the Harboøre gasiﬁer. The ﬁgure is from Lundtorp
(2008).
==PAGE:32==
8
CHAPTER 2. THE BIGCC POWER PLANT
As seen in ﬁgure 2.2 the air is fed to the gasiﬁer with an air compressor through a humidiﬁer and an
air heater. A part stream from the ﬂue gas condensate is used for humidifying the air. Heat is required to
evaporate the water in the humidiﬁer and superheating it to 150◦C in the air heater.
2.2.2
Gas cleaning
The gas cleaning system is of the same kind as in Harboøre. It is comprised of a gas cooler (one or two)
and a wet electrostatic precipitator (ESP). In the gas cooler the gas is cooled from 75-35◦C and water and tar
are condensed out. In the wet ESP the remaining tar and water droplets are collected and removed from the
gas. In Harboøre this cleaning process is effective and the remaining tar and dust are each below 25 mg/Nm3
(Teislev (2002)).
The gas booster raises the pressure of the product gas to a level suitable for the gas engines and controls
the production of product gas in the gasiﬁer.
The light tar is dissolved in the water condensate and called tarwater. Both the tarwater and the heavy tar
are directed to the tar storage tank.
2.2.3
Tarwater treatment
In the tar storage tank the tarwater and heavy tar are collected from the gas cooler and the wet ESP. The
heavy tar is separated by gravity and sinks to the bottom while the tarwater stays on top. The tarwater is
pumped by a circulation pump out of the tank and either back into it again or through the gas condensate
heater and than back into the tar storage tank. The pressure in the system (the tank and the heater) is kept
below atmospheric or at 0.76 bar. The water in the tarwater is boiled in the heater and at this pressure the
evaporating temperature of water is at 92◦C.
The steam collected in the storage tank is evacuated with the steam booster and led to the furnace. The
tar is pumped from the bottom and to the furnace as well. The steam booster keeps the pressure in the tank
at the desired level and the tarwater circulation system controls the condensate level in the storage tank.
2.2.4
Gas engines
It is expected that two gas engines are needed for the power plant and they should be suitable for
gasiﬁcation gas. The Jenbacher 300, 400 or 600 series could be considered as candidates. The main product
of the engine is electrical power but the cooling water is also a valuable product and can be used to heat up
streams within the power plant. The exhaust gases are sent to the furnace and serve as an oxidizer for the
combustion process.
2.2.5
Adiabatic furnace
The tar from the storage tank is injected into the adiabatic furnace and atomized into droplets of a size
that will allow it to burn out. The steam from the storage tank is injected to the furnace as well and the
exhaust gas from the engines is used as an oxidizer. If the ﬂow of tar is not adequate the product gas can be
bypassed into the furnace and combusted. The product of the furnace is the hot ﬂue gas which is led to the
heat recovery steam generator.
2.2.6
Steam cycle
The ﬂue gas from the furnace is cooled in the heat recovery steam generator (HRSG) to produce steam
in the steam cycle. The steam is preheated in an economizer until it has reached the state of a saturated
==PAGE:33==
2.3. ORC
9
liquid. In the evaporator the water is evaporated at 45 bar. Before the steam is delivered to the turbine it is
superheated in the superheater up to 450◦C. The steam is expanded in the turbine and mechanical power
is produced. The shaft power is converted to electrical power in a generator. The steam coming from the
turbine is condensed in an air cooled steam condenser operated at 0.1 bar. The condensation temperature
at this pressure is around 45◦C. The low pressure pump brings the pressure up to a level suitable for the
deaerator (1.1 bar). The condensate is preheated in the feedwater preheater up to 93◦C before it is sent to
the deaerator where leakage air is removed from the feedwater. The deaerating steam is extracted from the
HRSG. The high pressure pump brings the pressure level of the feedwater to the pressure of the HRSG.
2.2.7
Flue gas handling
The ﬂue gas from the HRSG can be cooled in a ﬂue gas cooler before it is sent to the stack. This is not
necessary unless there is demand for the heat. However, it is assumed in this study that the ﬂue gas is always
cooled down to 60◦C and a part of the condensate stream is used to humidify the gasiﬁcation air. The off
gases are carried by the ﬂue gas duct to an ID-fan which controls the pressure in the adiabatic furnace and
blows the gases to the stack.
In the concept design by Vølund the ﬂue gas is expected to be cooled in a wet scrubber. In this project
the scrubber is replaced by a simple gas cooler, identical to the product gas cooler.
2.2.8
Heat exchanger network
There are quite many heat needs and heat sources in the BIGCC. Vølund has already developed strategies
to use the waste heat for the internal heat needs of the power plant. The way this heat exchanger network
(HEN) is put together is sensitive to the design point chosen for the power plant. Therefore it is regarded as
one of the tasks of this study to design HENs for each of the proposed design cases in subsequent chapters.
2.3
ORC
An attempt is be made to integrate an organic Rankine cycle (ORC) to the BIGCC in several ways during
the design improvement phase of this project. This is done in chapter 5 and a description of the technology
is found in the same chapter.
==PAGE:34==
==PAGE:35==
CHAPTER 3
DNA model
In this chapter the modeling of the BIGCC is explained. The power plant is modeled using the simulation
tool DNA (Dynamic network analysis). It is a tool for energy systems analysis and has been developed by
the Department of Mechanical Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark.
Firstly, the target data, mostly provided by Vølund, is given. Secondly, the model is explained in details
and input data is given for each component. Assumptions and simpliﬁcations are also argued and explained
where needed. Finally, the model results for the basic design of the power plant, which is based on the target
data, is given and discussed.
3.1
Target data
The target data for the plant describes some of the desired inputs or outputs of the DNA model.
3.1.1
Wood chips
In table 3.1 the target data for the wood chips is shown. This data is given in Ahrenfeldt et al. (2006)
where the chemical composition of wood chips used in a gasiﬁer was measured. The average moisture
content of the wood chips should be 42% since it is a typical value for the fuel used at Harboøre. The
speciﬁc heat of the wood has to be speciﬁed in the gasiﬁer model and a reference for that can be found in
Radmanovic et al. (2014). The LHV of the wood chips is retrieved from two different sources; one on dry
basis and the other one on a wet basis. The value given by Vølund (wet basis) will be used as a leading target
value.

3.1.3
Tar
The tar amount in the product gas is a big uncertainty when modeling the plant. The limited data available
on the tar amount in the product gas from the Harboøre gasiﬁer shows large ﬂuctuations in both light and
heavy tar. A some sort of average could be around 50 g/Nm3(dry) of light tar and the same amount of heavy
tar. The tar output has a big inﬂuence on the performance of the system as shown in section 3.3.2.
The chemical composition of the tars is also uncertain to some degree. A fairly good approximation
is to model the heavy tar as phenol (C6H5OH) and the light tar as acetic acid (CH3COOH). The average
composition and the caloriﬁc values of the tars is very similar to those two compounds.

Other plant data

gas engine provided by Vølund. The measured data in the manual was found for an engine running on wood
gas and the results relevant for this study are shown in table 3.4.

3.2. SYSTEM MODELING

3.2
System modeling
The modeling approach of the entire power plant is described and the input data for each component is
given. The target data is used as input data where applicable.
In this basic model a heat exchanger network, connecting heat sources and heat needs, has not been
established. Hence, the heat in the heat sources is all wasted and the heat for the heat needs is retrieved from
an arbitrary source.
A diagram of the whole model with all components and node numbers is given in appendix A
3.2.1
Gasiﬁer
In ﬁgure 3.1 the components and nodes related to the gasiﬁer are shown. The DNA components used to
build this subsystem are:

Figure 3.1: DNA model of the gasiﬁer.
The gasiﬁer (Gasiﬁer) is modeled with three input streams and ﬁve output streams. The inputs are wood,
steam and air and the outputs are product gas, light tar, heavy tar, ash and heat.
==PAGE:40==
16
CHAPTER 3. DNA MODEL
Often gasiﬁers are modeled as Gibbs reactors. Then the mole fractions in the product gas are calculated
by minimizing the Gibbs free energy at a predeﬁned equilibrium temperature and pressure. Since the
chemical reactions in an updraft gasiﬁer hardly reach equilibrium, this is not considered a feasible modeling
technique in this case.
The gasiﬁer employed here allows for the composition of the product gas and the tars to be deﬁned
whereas the wood composition is retrieved as an output of the calculations. The gasiﬁer itself does not
allow for the wood composition to be calculated in this way but by connecting it to the solid generator
(solid_generator) it is possible to generate the wood composition that meets the conditions of the gasiﬁer.
The mass ﬂow of steam in node 3 is deﬁned as zero but instead the air in node 2 is humidiﬁed. Air enters
the gasiﬁer at 150◦C. Before it is heated to this temperature, it is saturated with steam and the saturation
temperature controls the amount of steam in the air. According to Teislev (2001), the humidiﬁcation
temperature should be around 65◦C. This will not necessarily be the case in the model since the temperature
is controlled by the amount of steam needed for the chemical reactions in the gasiﬁer. Another factor that
controls the amount of steam needed is that the product gas should be water-saturated when it leaves the
gasiﬁer.
In the tar mixer (TarMixer) the light and heavy tars are mixed together.
The HHV of the wood and tars is set or calculated in the solid generator model. The calculation is
achieved by the following correlation from Channiwala and Parikh (2002)
HHV [MJ/kg (dry)] = 0.3491C + 1.1783H + 0.1005S −0.1034O −0.0151N −0.0211ASH,
(3.1)
where the units of C, H, S, O, N and ASH are wt% (dry).
The main input data for the gasiﬁer is listed in table 3.5.

exactly the target data.

3.2.2
Product gas cooling
In ﬁgure 3.2 the components and nodes of the gas cooling system is shown. It is assumed that only one

Figure 3.2: DNA model of the gas cooler.
The gas cooler (GasCooler) is air cooled. In the real plant the tars are in the product gas and would be
condensed out with the water. Since the tars are in a separate stream in the model, the heat of condensation
of the tars does not add to that of the water. With reference to this the following assumptions are made:
• After condensing in the cooler and cleaning in the wet ESP the gas is completely free of tar. This
is a valid assumption since the gas cleaning system is effective enough to leave only small traces of
organic material.
==PAGE:43==
3.2. SYSTEM MODELING
19
• It is sufﬁcient to only account for the condensation heat of water in the gas cooler. According to the
results that will be presented later in this chapter, 0.05 kg/s of each tar type will be produced in the
gasiﬁer and 0.5 kg/s of water will be condensed in the gas cooler. The latent heat of vaporization for
those three liquids are:
Light tar (acetic acid):
389.01 kJ/kg (at 25◦C)
Heavy tar (phenol):
614.39 kJ/kg (at 25◦C)
Water:
2440.62 kJ/kg (at 25◦C)
This means that the heat from the condensation of water will be around 1220 kJ/s and the heat from
condensing all the tars will be around 50 kJ/s. Of course there is some sensible heat as well but it is
very minor compared to the condensation heat. From these rough calculations it is concluded that it is
a fair assumption to disregard the condensation heat of tars in the gas cooler.

Figure 3.3: DNA model of the gas engine.
The gas booster (Booster) brings the pressure of the product gas to a level adequate for the gas engine.
The Air fuel mixer (AirFuelMixer) mixes the product gas with combustion air before it is burned in the gas
engine (Engine). Although it is expected that two engines will be used in the power plant, only one (larger
one) is modeled. The set_x component is used to set the oxygen level in the engine ﬂue gas at 6%. The
Heat_split splits the recoverable heat into four components (see node descriptions).

In ﬁgure 3.5 the components and nodes of the tarwater treatment system are shown. The components

Water from heat source
In the tarwater treatment system the tarwater (or just water in the model) is preheated and evaporated in
a vacuum. The tar storage tank should be a part of this system but since the power plant is modeled in steady
state the tank does not serve a purpose in the model. It is therefore disregarded.
It is assumed that it is sufﬁcient to account only for the heat needed to heat and evaporate the water in
the tarwater, i.e. neglect the heating of the tar. The argument from section 3.2.2 regarding the condensation
of the tarwater in the gas cooler, also applies for this matter.
The condensate heater is split into a preheater and an evaporator. The tarwater pump (tw_pump)
pumps the condensate to the tarwater preheater (TarWaterPreHeat) where it is heated to the evaporating
==PAGE:47==
3.2. SYSTEM MODELING
23
Figure 3.5: DNA model of the tarwater treatment system.
temperature. It is assumed that a small fraction of the water remains in the tar (is not evaporated). In
the water splitter (WaterSplit) this fraction is directed straight to the furnace and the rest goes through
the water throttle (WaterThrottle), where the pressure is dropped, before it enters the tarwater evaporator
(TarWaterEvap). The saturated steam ﬂowing from the evaporator outlet is compressed to the furnace
pressure in the steam booster (SteamBooster). The PresRegWater valve is only present to add an equation
to the thermodynamic model.

340-342, 399, 308-309
Heat loss
401
Mechanical power
499, 411-412
Electrical power
Figure 3.7: DNA model of the steam cycle.
The ﬂue gas from the furnace is sent to the HRSG where it is used to preheat, evaporate and superheat
the steam in the economizer (ECO), evaporator (EVA) and superheater (SH), respectively. A small fraction
of the saturated steam at the evaporator outlet is extracted to the deaerator (Deaerator) through the steam
splitter (SplitSteam).
The superheated steam is directed to the turbine (Turbine) where the enthalpy is dropped to produce
mechanical power. The turbine shaft rotates the generator (generator) where the shaft power is converted to
electrical power. The air cooled condenser (Cond) is maintained at vacuum conditions and at the outlet the
steam has fully condensed to a saturated liquid.
The low pressure pump (LP_Pump) raises the pressure of the condensate to the deaerator pressure. In
the Feedwater preaheater (FeedwaterPreheater) the water is preheated before it enters the deaerator and in
the high pressure pump (HP_Pump) the pressure of the condensate is increased to the boiler conditions.
The main inputs for the steam cycle are shown in table 3.12. The isentropic efﬁciency of the turbine is
adjusted to get a similar power output as given in the target data.

Figure 3.8: DNA model of the ﬂue gas cooler.
In the model it is assumed that the ﬂue gas is always cooled down to 60◦C in the ﬂue gas cooler
(FlueGasCooler), regardless of if there exists a matching heat need or not. The ﬂue gases start to condensate
at 64◦C so there is a signiﬁcant amount of latent heat released as well as sensible heat. In real life the
condensation system would perhaps be of a different kind, e.g. a wet scrubber. The situation is simpliﬁed by
assuming that a regular gas cooler can be used.
The ﬂue gas fan (FlueGasFan) blows the ﬂue gas to the stack. In the ﬂue gas condensate splitter
(FlueGasCondSplit) the condensate from the ﬂue gas cooler is split to two streams. One stream goes to the
humidiﬁer and the other is wasted.

In ﬁgure 3.9 the components and nodes of the humidiﬁer and air heater are shown. The components used

The ambient air is deﬁned in the humid_air component. The relative humidity of the air is deﬁned as
70% because it resembles the average humidity in Italy (World Weather & Climate Information (2015)). In
the ambient air compressor (AmbAirComp) the air is compressed to overcome the pressure drops in the
humidiﬁer, air heater and gasiﬁer. In the ﬂue gas condensate pump (FlueCondPump) the water pressure is
raised to overcome these same pressure drops.
The humidiﬁer is modeled as a spray humidiﬁer which means that hot water is sprayed into a hot air
ﬂow and the heat needed to evaporate the water spray is extracted from the air itself. The humidiﬁer is
represented by the following components: humidiﬁer_1, humidiﬁer_2, splitter and FlueCondPreHeat. In
humidiﬁer_1 the air is prehumidiﬁed and is water-saturated at the outlet. In humidiﬁer_2 the air is heated to
the humidiﬁcation temperature and is water-saturated at the outlet. The humidiﬁcation temperature controls
how much steam is added to the air (see section 3.2.1). The reason why two humidifying components are
used, rather than one, is that it allows one to see the temperature of the saturated air in node 824.
In the loop 825-826-826-802-803, water is circulated. The amount of water that is extracted from the
loop in humidiﬁer_1 and humidiﬁer_2 is made up by ﬂue gas condensate water.
No pressure drop is modeled inside the humidiﬁer. Instead the pressure is lowered over the ambient air
valve (AmbAirValve) and ﬂue gas condensate valve (FlueCondValve).
==PAGE:53==
3.2. SYSTEM MODELING
29
The air heater (GasAirPreHeat) heats the humidiﬁed air to 150◦C and the valves PresSepGasAir and
PresSep are only present to add equations to the thermodynamic model.

3.3
Model results
The main model results for the basic case with approximately 4 MW net power output are shown in
tables 3.15, 3.16 and 3.17. The results are considered to be in reasonably good correspondence with the
target values. The power production is very close to the target value. The heat from the gas cooler is a
bit lower than the target value, but that can be explained, partly at least, by the fact that the cooling and
condensation of the tars is not accounted for. The engine waste heat is also considerably lower than the
target value but no measures are taken to rectify that result since the engine is modeled exactly according to
experimental data. The DNA model for this case is given in appendix E along with detailed model results.

It is expected that the auxiliary equipment uses electricity produced in the power plant and that is why
the auxiliary electricity consumption is subtracted in the numerator in equations (3.8) and (3.10). It should
==PAGE:56==
32
CHAPTER 3. DNA MODEL
also be noted that it is assumed that all heat needs of the power plant can be served by internal heat sources.
Therefore, no external heaters are involved in the efﬁciency calculations.
The T-Q diagram for the HRSG is shown in ﬁgure 3.10. The pinch point is located where the evaporator
and economizer meet and the pinch point temperature difference is around 80◦C. This value is quite high
considering that the optimum ∆Tpinch, with regards to cost and heat transfer, is usually considered to be
around 10◦C. Measures could be taken to lower this value by either lowering the output temperature of the
ﬂue gas or by increasing the pressure on the steam side.
Figure 3.10: T-Q diagram for the HRSG in the 4 MW plant.
Figure 3.11 sheds light on how the auxiliary power consumption is distributed between pumps and
compressors in the power plant. The main consumers are identiﬁed as the steam booster and the ﬂue gas fan,
with around 3/4 of the total power consumption.

3.3.1
Heat sources and heat needs
The heat needs and heat sources are of great importance in this project since the intension is to investigate
the feasibility of using excess heat sources for electricity production in a low-temperature power cycle. In
table 3.18 the heat sources and heat needs are gathered.
==PAGE:57==
3.3. MODEL RESULTS
33
The HRSG heat exchangers do not enter this table and will generally not be a part of other similar tables
in subsequent chapters. The reason is that these heat exchangers are already connected to the ﬂue gas on the
hot side and it is assumed that no further heat exchangers will be integrated into the HRSG itself.
The sum of heat sources is more than two times higher than the sum of heat needs. The largest part
of the heat sources is however at a rather low temperature. The only heat source hot enough to evaporate
the tarwater is the ﬂue gas. The ﬂue gas can deliver 1219 kW where around 400 kW stem from the water
condensation at a temperature below 64◦C. The sensible heat above 102◦C in the ﬂue gas can be used to
evaporate the tarwater, assuming ∆Tpinch = 10◦C in the tarwater evaporator. This heat amounts to around
550 kW which is only about half of what is needed.

In light of the above discussion it is evident that some design changes need to be made before an ORC
unit is integrated with the power plant. These changes should aim at a) lowering the pinch point temperature
difference in the HRSG and b) ensure that the heat needs can be covered with the heat sources.
The design point of the power plant should be near it’s optimum before any attempts are made to utilize
the waste heat in a low-temperature power cycle.
3.3.2
Effect of varying the tar amount on the design point performance
The biggest uncertainty in the model stems from the tar amount produced in the gasiﬁer. The tar
production has been seen to be unsteady in the Harboøre gasiﬁer and a reliable average value is not at hand
due to scarce data. It is therefore interesting to see how changes in the tar amount effect the design point
performance of the power plant.
Figure 3.12 shows how the design point efﬁciency and engine power vary with the tar concentration in
the product gas. Lowering the tar amount increases the engine power and consequently the efﬁciency. The
design parameters of the steam cycle are constant. The reason for the increased engine power is that less
wood is converted to tar and more is converted to gas which fuels the engine.

A T-Q diagram for the HRSG with various tar amounts is shown in ﬁgure 3.13. The main effect of

gas when leaving the furnace.
Figure 3.13: T-Q diagram for the HRSG in the 4 MW plant over a range of different tar concentrations. The
design point chosen is at 100 g/Nm3(dry).
From the ﬁgures it can be concluded that a lower tar amount in the product gas has a positive effect on
the performance of the power plant. It is the nature of updraft gasiﬁers to produce a substantial amount of tar
so it cannot be avoided. However, it is very beneﬁcial to have a good estimate on the average amount of tar
produced in the gasiﬁer to be able to deﬁne a realistic design point for the power plant. To be able to do that,
more data needs to be collected.
==PAGE:59==
CHAPTER 4
Case A - 5 MW plant
Although the data given by Vølund was for a 4 MW power plant the intension is to scale it up to 5 MW
net power output. This is the objective of this chapter.
The same inputs are used in this case as in the 4 MW case with three exceptions:
• The isentropic efﬁciency of the steam turbine is raised from 70% to 80%. This is done because in
the 5 MW design the power output of the turbine is expected to be higher then in the 4 MW case. A
larger turbine will therefore be used and it is assumed that due to the size increase the efﬁciency can
be expected to be higher.
• The wood ﬂow is increased.
• The steam ﬂow in the steam cycle is increased.
A major task in this chapter is to design a heat exchanger network for the power plant. This involves
developing an efﬁcient conﬁguration of heat exchangers that allow heat needs to be matched with heat
sources. The method applied to achieve this is called pinch analysis or pinch technology. The ﬁrst section in
this chapter describes this method.
4.1
Pinch analysis
Pinch analysis is a technique for optimizing heat exchanger networks (HENs). For a given number of hot
and cold process streams the number of possible HEN conﬁgurations is
Nhen = (Nh × Nc)!
(4.1)
where Nh is the number of hot streams, Nc is the number of cold streams and Nhen is the number of
HENs. Not all combinations are applicable solutions but this equation underlines that the possibilities can be
quite many. It would be a time consuming task to test a large number of different solutions and to avoid that,
pinch analysis creates a framework which helps the designer to identify a cost-effective solution.
The use of the method can vary slightly between designers and applications. Two assumptions are
however always applied:
• All heat exchangers in the network are assumed to be of the counter-ﬂow type.
• The average speciﬁc heat of each stream is assumed to be constant.
35
==PAGE:60==
36
CHAPTER 4. CASE A - 5 MW PLANT
The pinch analysis approach taken here can be described in the following way:
1. Identify the process streams, their temperatures and heat capacity rates (CPs). The CP has the
units kW/K and is the mass ﬂow times the speciﬁc heat - or if this information is not at hand, the heat
input (cold stream) or output (hot stream) divided by the temperature span of the stream.
2. Draw the composite curves and ﬁnd the process pinch. The process streams are classiﬁed as hot
and cold process streams. The hot streams represent the heat sources and the cold streams represent the
heet needs. The construction of the hot and cold composite curves is best explained with an example
adapted from Bejan et al. (1996). In table 4.1 four process streams are given.
Table 4.1: Heat sources and heat needs for the pinch analysis example.

Each temperature interval represents a straight line where the
X

X

are made. This can be seen in ﬁgure 4.1. In fact, the curves can be shifted horizontally as one pleases
because the absolute values on the horizontal axis do not have a meaning - only their differences from
one point to another. The usual practice is to draw the hot composite curve starting at zero on the
horizontal axis. Then the cold composite curve is moved horizontally and placed so that the minimum
temperature difference (∆Tmin) is equal to some appropriate value. This value is selected as 10◦C in
this study. Figure 4.1 illustrates this.
==PAGE:61==
4.1. PINCH ANALYSIS
37
The location of the minimum temperature difference is called the process pinch. The temperature of
the cold composite curve and the hot composite curve at the process pinch are called Tcc,pinch and
Thc,pinch, respectively.
Figure 4.1: Composite curves for the pinch analysis example.
3. Design of the HEN. The maximum energy recovery (MER) is shown in ﬁgure 4.1. For the chosen
∆Tmin there is also some need for external cooling and heating. According to the pinch analysis
methodology, MER is only achieved if the following rules are obeyed:
• Heat should not be transferred across the pinch.
• A hot utility should only be used above the pinch.
• A cold utility should only be used below the pinch.
It leads from these rules that separate networks should be designed above and below the pinch for a
MER design. Furthermore, the following set of equations should be satisﬁed simultaneously
Above the pinch:
(Nh)pinch ≤(Nc)pinch
(4.3)
CPh ≤CPc
(4.4)
Below the pinch:
(Nh)pinch ≥(Nc)pinch
(4.5)
CPh ≥CPc
(4.6)
If some of these equations cannot be satisﬁed, splitting of streams might be inevitable.
In subsequent chapters three HENs are designed. A MER design is the ultimate goal and the rules of
pinch analysis are respected at all times. Moreover, although ∆Tmin = 10◦C, the minimum temperature
difference in the heat exchangers in the network are decided based on the kind of ﬂow material exchanging
heat in them. The rules of thumb followed here on this matter are:
Gas-Gas:
∆Tpinch = 15◦C
Gas-Liquid:
∆Tpinch = 10◦C
Liquid-Liquid:
∆Tpinch = 5◦C
Finally, some of the good advices given in Bejan et al. (1996) regarding the design of HENs are kept in
mind. These advices are not repeated here since they are not necessarily an essential part of pinch analysis
but rather some common practice based on design experience.
==PAGE:62==
38
CHAPTER 4. CASE A - 5 MW PLANT
4.2
The ﬂue gas heat source
Cooling a ﬂue gas can be a tricky task. The acid dew point in the gas usually sets the lower limit for the
ﬂue gas temperature in a regular heat exchanger. When acids are deposited on metal surfaces, corrosion is
inevitable. Vølund has encountered problems with hydrochloric acid in their ﬂue gas pipes and sulfuric acids
are always a threat. Using special materials, e.g. coating on heat exchanger surfaces, is an option but an
expensive one. An alternative is to use some kind of a de-sulfurizer, e.g. dry or wet scrubber.
It is important to know the dew points of the acids in a ﬂue gas. By having this knowledge the utilization
of the waste heat can be maximized but still keeping the temperature safely above the dew point.
The amount of sulfuric acid in the ﬂue gas from Harboøre, is about 16 ppm. According to model results
for the BIGCC it is 22 ppm. The sulfuric acid amount is dependent on the amount of sulfur in the fuel.
Usually only traces of sulfur are found in wood biomass. The amount in coal is much higher, for example.
The chlorine often stems from a secondary source, such as seawater that has been sprayed over the wood
logs to keep them moisturized (Ahrenfeldt et al. (2006)).
The acid dew point in the ﬂue gas from the BIGCC is not known. When designing the steam cycle
and the HEN it is assumed that acid condensation is not a problem. Either that it does not occur or that it
is dealt with by some means. According to Quoilin et al. (2013), research is being carried out to design
HEXs that can withstand and evacuate acid condensates. Perhaps, at the time when the BIGCC will be built,
economically feasible HEXs of this kind will be available.
4.3
Choosing the design point
There are two variables that can be changed to achieve a higher power output: the ﬂow of wood into the
gasiﬁer and the steam ﬂow in the steam cycle. In other words: to increase the power output either the engine
needs to produce more power or the steam turbine - or both. At this point the thermodynamic parameters
of the steam cycle (such as evaporating pressure) are not varied and therefore the only way to increase the
power output of the turbine is to have more steam ﬂowing in the cycle and consequently pulling down the
pinch point temperature difference.
When scaling up the power plant the question arises if optimum values exist for these two variables that
can maximize the efﬁciency? It is clear that with a lower pinch temperature difference in the HRSG, more
heat could be extracted from the ﬂue gas to produce power in the steam cycle. Hence it can be stated that
setting ∆Tpinch = 10◦C (assumed to be the most cost-effective value for the HRSG) maximizes the overall
efﬁciency. This cannot be achieved, however.
The heat sources have to be able to cover the heat needs. This means that the temperature of the ﬂue
gas can be lowered in the HRSG down to the point where the high temperature heat sources match the high
temperature heat needs. By making a preliminary pinch analysis (using some good guess values for the wood
and steam ﬂows) the process pinch was be estimated. It was found that the Tcc,pinch was equal to 75◦C.
A parametric study was made for the power plant where various combinations of wood and steam ﬂow
were used. For each model run the following was calculated:
• Electrical efﬁciency.
• Net power production.
• HRSG pinch point temperature difference.
• Heat needs above pinch point.
==PAGE:63==
4.3. CHOOSING THE DESIGN POINT
39
• Heat sources above pinch point.
A valid combination of wood and steam ﬂow is when the heat sources above the pinch point can cover
the heat needs above pinch point. The optimum design point is the valid combination with the maximum
efﬁciency and around 5 MW output. This design point was found graphically with the aid of ﬁgures 4.2 and
4.3.
In ﬁgure 4.3b the heat sources above the pinch point are shown with the colored lines and the heat needs
above the pinch point are shown with the dashed black line. The valid design points are above the dashed
black line. From ﬁgure 4.2b the design points that give a 5 MW output can be identiﬁed and in ﬁgure 4.2a the
efﬁciencies of these design points can be compared. In ﬁgure 4.3a it is veriﬁed that lower pinch temperature
difference in the HRSG is followed by a higher efﬁciency of the power plant.
From the ﬁgures it can be concluded that the optimum design is with:

In ﬁgure 4.5 the composite curves for the process streams are drawn. The process pinch is located at

It is clear that all heat needs can be covered by the heat sources. No external heating is needed and
the reason is the choice of design point. The ﬂue gas is cooled in the HRSG down to the point where heat
sources above the pinch point can be exactly matched by the heat needs above the pinch point.
The horizontal part of the cold composite curve in ﬁgure 4.5 represents the tarwater evaporator. When
looking at the ﬁgure the idea emerges that by lowering the evaporating temperature of the tarwater the
evaporation could by achieved, at least partly, with some of the colder heat sources - mainly the engine
cooling water. This design change would lead to the obvious beneﬁt that the ﬂue gas (the steepest part of the
hot composite curve) could be cooled even further in the HRSG, leading to more power production in the
steam cycle and increased efﬁciency of the whole power plant.
In design alternatives B, and C two different versions of this change will be modeled and new HENs will
be designed for each case.

CHAPTER 5
Plant improvements
The objective of the work presented in this chapter is to ﬁnd ways to improve the electrical efﬁciency of
the power plant. The aim is in general to make better use of the waste heat within the power plant in order to
generate more power for the same fuel input.
Six design cases will be analyzed:
1. Case B. The tar storage tank pressure is lowered to a value that optimizes the electrical efﬁciency.
This is done in order to use some of the low temperature heat sources to evaporate the tarwater, partly
at least, instead of using the ﬂue gas for that. The ﬂue gas can in turn be cooled further in the HRSG
which increases the power output of the steam cycle.
The ﬂue gas can though only be cooled to 165◦C in the HRSG because it must also be used to heat up
the gasiﬁcation air to 150◦C.
2. Case C. The same as case B except that the gasiﬁcation air is heated with the ﬂue gas from the engine.
This arrangement makes it possible to cool the ﬂue gas down to 113◦C in the HRSG.
3. Case C2. The same as case C except that the live steam pressure and temperature in the steam cycle
are optimized.
4. Case D. An ORC unit is integrated with two of the design cases.
a) Case DA1. An ORC unit is integrated with design case A. One evaporator is used.
b) Case DA2. An ORC unit is integrated with design case A. Two evaporators connected in parallel
are used.
c) Case DB. An ORC unit is integrated with design case B.
The economic feasibility of the different design options will be evaluated in chapter 6. Thermoeconomic
analysis will be carried out and the levelized cost of electricity will be calculated for all design cases.
47
==PAGE:72==
48
CHAPTER 5. PLANT IMPROVEMENTS
5.1
Case B
It is not obvious what the optimum tar storage tank pressure is for this case. A parametric study for this
parameter alone is not sufﬁcient. Changes in the steam cycle and the HEN design will have to be considered
simultaneously to estimate the optimum value.
The methodology for ﬁnding the optimum tar storage tank pressure is explained as follows:
1. Use the results for the heat sources and heat needs from case A as a starting point. Only the load of
the ﬂue gas heat source is assumed to change during the design changes described in the next steps.
Other sources and needs will also vary a bit but that can be neglected.
2. Vary the storage tank pressure and calculate the corresponding evaporating temperatures from the
Clausius-Clapeyron equation
ln
p2
p1

sat
≈hfg
R
 1
T1
−1
T2

sat
(5.1)
where the variables p1 = 0.5 bar and T1 = 354.5 K are a known saturation pressure and saturation
temperature pair and p2 is the saturation pressure for which the saturation temperature T2 needs to
be determined. The latent heat of vaporization of water (at p1) is hfg = 2304.7 kJ/kg and the gas
constant for water is R = 0.4615 kJ/(kg·K).
3. Assume that the cold stream process pinch temperature (Tcc,pinch) will be at the tarwater evaporating
temperature. This assumption will not hold true for evaporating temperatures higher than 85◦C but
can nevertheless be used without effecting the results of this investigation.
4. Calculate the heat needs above pinch point.
5. Calculate the available heat from all heat sources except the ﬂue gas above pinch point. For this a
minimum temperature difference of 10◦C and 5◦C for gas and liquid sources, respectively, is used.
6. The additional heat needed to cover the heat needs is calculated. This heat must come from the ﬂue gas
and therefore the minimum allowed temperature of the ﬂue gas at the HRSG outlet can be calculated.
165◦C is used as an absolute minimum to be able to heat up the gasiﬁcation air with the ﬂue gas.
7. Finally, a parametric study can be conducted using the DNA model from case A. The parameters that
need to be varied simultaneously are:
• The tarwater storage tank pressure.
• The outlet temperature of the (tar)water in the tarwater preheater.
• The temperature of the ﬂue gas at the HRSG outlet.
The results from steps 1-6 in the method description are given in table C.1 in appendix C.
The results from the parametric study are shown in ﬁgures 5.1 and 5.2. In ﬁgure 5.1a the HRSG pinch
temperature difference is shown. It is not until the storage tank pressure is down to 0.45 bar that other heat
sources than the ﬂue gas can be used to evaporate the tarwater. As the pressure is lowered further the share
of other heat sources in evaporating the tarwater gets larger. As a consequence the HRSG pinch temperature
difference can be lowered. It is lowered until the lower boundary is reached, i.e. where the ﬂue gas outlet
temperature is down to 165◦C.
==PAGE:73==
5.1. CASE B
49
The steam booster power increases as the pressure in the tank is lowered. This happens because the
pressure difference across the booster is increased. A consequence of the increased booster power is that
the temperature of the superheated steam coming out of the booster is raised. This causes an increase in
the temperature of the ﬂue gas from the furnace as well. That effects the heat input from the ﬂue gas into
the steam cycle. This effect is the reason why the turbine power, seen in ﬁgure 5.2b is higher at 0.1 bar
than 0.3 bar despite that the pinch temperature difference in the HRSG is constant. The same effect is also
responsible for the decreasing turbine power from 0.5-0.76 bar. The sharp descend of the turbine power
curve in the 0.32-0.5 bar range is due to the steep increase of the pinch temperature difference in the HRSG
in the same range.
The combined effect of the the variations in the steam booster power and turbine power is witnessed
in ﬁgure 5.1b where the efﬁciency is shown. The curve has a very apparent peak around 0.32 bar which
corresponds to an evaporating temperature of 70.4◦C. At this pressure the minimum temperature of the ﬂue
gas at the HRSG outlet is 165◦C. These values are therefore chosen as input variables for this design case.
(a) Boiler pinch.
(b) Electrical efﬁeciency.

(a) Steam booster power.
(b) Turbine power.

composite curve at the same end.

5.2
Model results
In table 5.4 the main model results for case B are given. Results for case A are also given for comparison.
The gray colored rows are where values have changed between design cases.
These results are from a model where the heat exchanger network is modeled as well. The main changes
from case A to case B can be outlined as follows:
• The power consumption of the steam booster has increased dramatically due to the higher pressure
difference across it. It is now by far the largest power consumer in the power plant. Differences in the
power consumption of other pumps and compressors (except for the LP and HP pumps) is due to the
design change of the HEN.
• The ﬂue gas temperature out of the furnace has increased slightly due to the higher temperature of the
superheated steam from the steam booster entering the furnace.
• The pinch temperature difference in the HRSG is lowered by around 90◦C and as a consequence the
power production in the steam cycle is increased.
• The wasted heat is lowered substantially.

5.3
Case C
Case C is solved in the same way as case B except that the air heater is removed from the list of heat
needs. It is decided beforehand to heat the air with the engine exhaust and therefore this heat need does not
have to be considered when designing the HEN.
In this case the air heater does no longer place a constraint on the minimum temperature of the ﬂue gas at
the HRSG outlet. Hence, the ﬂue gas can be cooled to 113◦C in the HRSG resulting in a ∆Tpinch = 10◦C.
This scenario gives another optimum for the storage tank pressure than in case B.
The results from steps 1-6 in the method description from section 5.1 are given in table C.2 in appendix
C. The results from the parametric study are shown in ﬁgures 5.9 and 5.10. The same trends are seen in the
ﬁgures as in Case B and the explanations given in section 5.1 also apply here. The optimum storage tank
pressure is now at 0.27 bar whereas the ﬂue gas temperature at the HRSG outlet is 113◦C. These values are
used as input variables for this design case.

5.3.3
Model results
In table 5.9 the main model results of case C are given. Results for case B are also given for comparison.

The main changes from case B to case C can be outlined as follows:

pumps) is due to the design change of the HEN.

superheated steam from the steam booster is higher in case C but the temperature of the exhaust gases
from the engine are lower when they enter the furnace because ﬁrst they are passed through the air
heater. The combined effect is a lower ﬂue gas temperature at the furnace outlet.
• The pinch temperature difference in the HRSG is lowered to it’s minimum value and as a consequence
the power production in the steam cycle is increased.
• The waste heat is reduced.

5.4
Case C2
In cases B and C the result of the design changes was to reduce the pinch point temperature difference in
the HRSG and consequently increasing the electrical efﬁciency of the power plant. Another way to increase
the efﬁciency is by modifying the steam cycle.
Several ways exist to improve the efﬁciency of a steam Rankine cycle and the same fundamental idea
lies behind all of these modiﬁcations:
• The average temperature at which heat is transferred to the working ﬂuid in the HRSG, Thigh,ave,
should be increased or the average temperature at which heat is rejected from the working ﬂuid,
Tlow,ave, should be decreased.
To lower Tlow,ave the condensing pressure can be decreased. Raising Thigh,ave can be achieved by e.g.:
1. Increasing the live steam pressure.
2. Increasing the live steam temperature.
3. Using a reheat Rankine cycle where the steam is expanded in two stages and reheated in between.
==PAGE:87==
5.4. CASE C2
63
4. Adding pressure levels in the HRSG.
In case C2 the effects of raising the live steam pressure and temperature are studied. In ﬁgure 5.17 the
effect of varying those parameters on the electrical efﬁciency and the moisture content at the turbine outlet
are examined. During these parametric studies the feedwater temperature is essentially constant and the ﬂue
gas outlet temperature as well - maintaining a constant pinch point temperature difference in the HRSG.
The efﬁciency turns out, as expected, to be highest for the highest pressure and temperature. The study is
done for pressures up to 100 bar and temperatures up to 500◦C. It depends on the ﬁnal selection of turbine
how much pressure and temperature can be tolerated. For instance, Siemens has a steam turbine with a
maximum output of 750 kW that can tolerate 101 bar and 500◦C and another one with a maximum output of
6 MW that can tolerate 131 bar and 530◦C (Siemens (2015)).
The moisture content at the turbine outlet must be kept within a certain limit to reduce the risk of erosion
in the last stage of the turbine. From ﬁgure 5.17a it can be seen that the combination of 100 bar and 500◦C
leads to a moisture content at the turbine outlet of around 10%. This is considered to be within limits and
these values are chosen as input parameters for case C2.

5.4.1
Model results
The heat exchanger network is virtually unaffected by the changes in the steam cycle. The only change
is that the load of the feedwater preheater is decreased by 6 kW. The HEN design from case C therefore also
applies for case C2.

5.5
Case D
The design cases that are presented in the previous sections all focus on modifying some parameters in
the BIGCC in order to make better use of of the waste heat internally and improve the electrical efﬁciency.
In case D the possibility of integrating a bottoming ORC to the plant is investigated.
5.5.1
ORC machines
The most widely used technology for low-grade heat recovery for power production is the ORC machine.
It utilizes waste heat to produce additional electricity. In principal the ORC is similar to the steam Rankine
cycle (SRC) and is made up of the same fundamental components: A boiler, an expansion device, a condenser
and a pump. The dissimilarities of the technologies relate to the different properties of the working ﬂuids.
The following discussion about ORCs relies on the excellent review article of Quoilin et al. (2013).
The organic ﬂuids used in an ORC have lower boiling temperatures than water and can therefore be
used to recover heat at a lower temperature. Moreover, the layout of an ORC is simpler than of the SRC.
Once through boilers can be used which means that the preheating, evaporating and superheating of the
working ﬂuid can be accomplished in one heat exchanger and no drums or recirculation is needed. This
is due to the fact that the density difference between the liquid and vapor phases of the organic ﬂuids is
considerably smaller than between liquid water and steam. This gives rise to higher differences of heat
transfer and pressure drop characteristics for liquid water and steam which creates a necessity of a more
involved boiler design.
Another major difference of ORCs and SRCs is that in the former there is normally no need for
superheating. In ﬁgure 5.19 a few typical ORC working ﬂuids are compared with water. The slope of the
right side of the saturation curve (vapor curve) of water is negative which is the reason why superheating is
an essential part of every SRC to avoid excessive droplets formation in the turbine. This gives rise to the
deﬁnition of water as a wet ﬂuid. Organic ﬂuids are classiﬁed as wet, isentropic or dry. Isentropic ﬂuids
have a nearly vertical vapor curve whereas dry ﬂuids have a positively sloping vapor curve. The wet organic
ﬂuids have a negatively sloping vapor curve like water but the slope is much steeper. In many cases this
almost eliminates the need for superheating since the working ﬂuid is usually superheated at the expander
outlet even if no superheating is applied.
Figure 5.19: Some typical ORC working ﬂuids compared with water. Figure from (Quoilin et al. (2013)).
Usually ORCs only incorporate the four fundamental components mentioned previously and possibly,
depending on the application, a recuperator. The recuperator heats up the feed with the superheated steam
==PAGE:91==
5.5. CASE D
67
from the expander and as a consequence less heat is needed in the boiler. This leads to improved efﬁciency
of the cycle. Figure 5.20 shows the layout of an ORC with and without a recuperator.
Figure 5.20: An ORC with and without a recuperator. Figure from (Quoilin et al. (2013)).
As for every other heat engine the efﬁciency of a Rankine cycle increases with a higher Tave,high or
a lower Tave,low. For an ORC, Tave,low has a lower limit controlled by the environment temperature and
Tave,high is controlled by the heat source. Hence, low temperature heat sources are utilized by lower
efﬁciency ORCs than medium or high temperature heat sources. For each application a lower bound of the
heat source temperature can be found where the ORC technology ceases to be economically feasible. It
means that due to the low efﬁciency the cost of electricity becomes too high. As an example it is stated in
Quoilin et al. (2013) that this lower bound for geothermal plants is around 80◦C.
5.5.2
Integration of an ORC with the BIGCC
In the previous cases the temperature of the heat sources when they have been used to cover the internal
heat needs, is not high. For case A, which has the largest share of waste heat of all the design cases, the
engine cooling water has a temperature of around 82◦C and the product gas in the gas cooler is at 75◦C.
The ﬂue gas only has a temperature of 69◦C. These low temperatures mean that an integration of an ORC
machine might not be economical.
Nevertheless, an attempt is made to integrate an ORC in an optimum way. When ORCs are used for waste
heat recovery (WHR) the objective is usually to maximize the net power output rather than the efﬁciency. In
this way the electrical efﬁciency of the whole power plant is maximized. This is the method of choice here.
The economical aspects of each proposed integration case are evaluated in chapter 6.
ORC layout
The pinch point temperature difference in the heat exchangers is of extreme importance for the outcome
of the analysis. Often heat transfer loops with thermal oil are used to transfer heat from the heat source
to the working ﬂuid. Another way is to connect the heat source directly to the ORC HEXs. It is assumed
here that the heat sources are directly connected since using a heat transfer loop would result in even lower
temperatures on the hot sides of the ORC HEXs. The minimum pinch point temperature difference is chosen
according to the rules of thumb presented in section 4.1. For the available heat sources these differences are:
Engine cooling water:
∆Tpinch = 5◦C
Product gas:
∆Tpinch = 10◦C
Flue gas:
∆Tpinch = 10◦C
==PAGE:92==
68
CHAPTER 5. PLANT IMPROVEMENTS
Although a once-through boiler could be used the ORC is modeled using a preheater and an evaporator.
The effects of superheating (in a separate superheater) is also investigated. The main ground for using a
preheater is the nature of the heat sources. The product gas is well suited for preheating while the other heat
sources are better suited for evaporating.
The use of a recuperator is not considered necessary for the following reasons:
• The heat source used for preheating, the product gas, is abundant. There is no need for saving it.
• Recuperators are only a part of the cycle when dry working ﬂuids are being used, i.e. when the slope
of the ﬂuid’s vapor curve is positive. Only one such ﬂuid is tested (see later in this section) and adding
a recuperator just for that ﬂuid is not considered necessary.
Working ﬂuid selection
Many organic ﬂuids exist that can be used as working ﬂuids in ORCs. It would be a tedious task to test
all of them for each application and therefore some pre-screening is necessary. To be able to do that in a
sensible way a selection criteria has to be established.
When selecting a working ﬂuid many aspects can be considered. In Quoilin et al. (2013) a list of 13
items that can effect the selection is presented. In this study the pre-selection is carried out based on the
following criteria:
• Supercritical ﬂuids are not considered.
• Mixtures of ﬂuids are not considered.
• The critical temperature of the ﬂuid should be lower than 200◦C. For most applications the ﬂuid
of choice has a critical temperature slightly higher than the evaporating temperature (Quoilin et al.
(2013)). If the critical temperature is much higher than the evaporating temperature the resulting
vapor densities will be overly low. Higher vapor density leads to larger volume ﬂows and invokes the
need for larger HEXs to limit the pressure drops. Hence, this is an economic condition rather than a
thermodynamic one.
• Zero- or nearly zero-ODP (ozone depleting potential) value.
• GWP (greenhouse warming potential) value below 1500.
• The ﬂuid must exist in the CoolProp property library since it is used when modeling the cycle in DNA.
Other conditions could be considered of course. For example concerning safety (toxicity and ﬂamma-
bility), commercial availability etc. These are important factors as well but are however overlooked in this
study.
The selected working ﬂuids are shown in table 5.13. These are the same working ﬂuids that were used in
a study on a WHR ORC (Quoilin et al. (2011)) with a few extra suggestions from Andreasen et al. (2014) and
Guo et al. (2011) where low temperature heat recovery was studied in both cases. In addition, all working
ﬂuids in the table fulﬁll the previously deﬁned selection criteria.
The ﬁnal selection of the most appropriate working ﬂuid was performed by ﬁxing the pinch point
temperature differences in the HEXs and running a parametric study where the evaporating pressure was
varied. An optimum pressure was found for each ﬂuid and the ﬂuid that gave the highest net power output
was chosen.

Modeling of the ORC
The ORC is modeled as a separate DNA model. The chemical composition and state properties of the
heat source ﬂuids are obtained from the BIGCC. To investigate the effects of the ORC on the BIGCC the
HEXs in the BIGCC were modiﬁed (split up to more number of units) according to the layout in the ORC
model. This means of doing this analysis is to simplify the modeling phase.
To obtain the properties of the working ﬂuids the property database CoolProp is used (Bell et al. (2014)).
Model drawings accompany each case of integration in later sections but a basic model is presented in ﬁgure
5.21. In the real designs the superheater is not always a part of the system and more number of evaporators
connected in parallel might be used. The node numbers might also be different.

Heat source ﬂuid
Figure 5.21: DNA model of an ORC.
The feed is preheated in the preheater (eco), evaporated in the evaporator (eva) and superheated in the
superheater (sup). The vapor is expanded in the turbine (orct) to produce work and condensed in the air
cooled condenser. To close the cycle the ﬂuid is pumped to the evaporating pressure in the pump_ORC.
The shaft power from the turbine is converted to electrical power in the generator (gen_ORC).

Efﬁciency of the ORC
The efﬁciency of the ORC is calculated as follows
ηorc = Pgen,orc −Ppump,orc
˙Qin
(5.2)
where ˙Qin is the sum of heat inputs to the cycle.
Effects of the ORC on the BIGCC
The ORC integration has minor effects on the operation of the BIGCC. They can be summed up as:
• The number of gas coolers increases and the temperature of the gas condensate entering the tarwater
pump and tarwater preheater is higher and the pressure drop in the gas increases.
• The tarwater pump and gas booster use more power.
• The heating load in the tarwater preheater is lower and therefore less heat is extracted from the heat
source connected to it.
==PAGE:96==
72
CHAPTER 5. PLANT IMPROVEMENTS
• The air cooler for the engine cooling water is replaced by an ORC evaporator.
• When the ﬂue gas is used as a heat source the ﬂue gas cooler is replaced by an ORC evaporator.
The efﬁciency of the BIGCC is of course affected by the ORC integration. The new efﬁciency can by
calculated as
ηel,bigcc+orc = Pout,bigcc −Paux,bigcc + Pgen,orc −Ppump,orc
˙Qwood
(5.3)

5.5. CASE D

Results
In this case there was no need for a dedicated parametric study. It was obvious by making a few test runs
that the optimum evaporating temperature for all working ﬂuids was
Tevap,DA1 = 65◦C
Raising this temperature had the effect that the mass ﬂow in the cycle was reduced which results in
less power output. Lowering it meant lower efﬁciency and decreased heat input in the preheater which also
results in less power output.
For this evaporating temperature four degrees of superheating were tested for all working ﬂuids. The
results can be seen in tables D.1-D.2 in appendix D. The net power output was highest for all working
ﬂuids when no superheating was applied. However, the efﬁciency was seen to increase for most of them
as the degree of superheating was increased. The ﬁve working ﬂuids with the highest net power output
are: Propylene, R1234yf, R134a, R290 and R32. R32 had the highest net power output but a considerable
amount of moisture at the turbine outlet (16.8%). Therefore the ﬂuid with the second highest net power
output (slightly lower than R32) is the ﬂuid of choice for this case. That ﬂuid is R1234yf and it had no
moisture at the turbine outlet.

5.5.4
Case DA2
In this case a second conﬁguration is tested for an ORC integration with design case A. Now the product
gas is not only used for preheating but also for evaporating. This is achieved by connecting two evaporators
in parallel where the product gas is the heat source in one of them and the engine cooling water in the other.
No superheating is assumed in this case since it did not turn out to be beneﬁcial in case DA1.
Results
In this case there was a good reason to do a parametric study to ﬁnd the optimum evaporating pressure.
When the pressure is lowered more heat can be extracted from the product gas for evaporation since the pinch
point temperature difference in the product gas evaporator is kept at 10◦C during the optimization. Another
effect when lowering the evaporating pressure, working in the opposite direction, is a decrease in the cycle
efﬁciency. The best results for all working ﬂuids is shown in table D.3 in appendix D. All working ﬂuids
have a maximum net power output around 60◦C except for ammonia where the maximum is around 58◦C.
The top ﬁve working ﬂuids are the same for this case as in case DA1 but the order is different. The ORC has
a maximum net power output when using R1234yf as a working ﬂuid at an evaporating temperature of

Results
As in case DA2 a parametric study was performed to ﬁnd the evaporating pressure that maximizes the
net power output. During this study the ﬂue gas temperature was allowed to go below 60◦C. The best results
for all working ﬂuids are shown in table D.4 in appendix D. All working ﬂuids had a maximum net power
output around 49◦C except for ammonia where the maximum was around 48◦C. The top ﬁve working ﬂuids
==PAGE:104==
80
CHAPTER 5. PLANT IMPROVEMENTS
are the same for this case as in case DA1 and DA2 and once again R1234yf gave the highest net power
output. The evaporating temperature for this case is therefore
Tevap,DB = 49◦C
This temperature is lower than is usually seen in commercial ORC units. In ﬁgure 5.28 the results of the
parametric study for R1234yf are shown. The curves for the other working ﬂuids are shaped in a similar way.
Figure 5.28: Evaporating pressure optimization for R1234yf. Case DB.
The integration of the ORC had the effect that the available heat in the engine cooling water heat source
increased slightly. The engine cooling water is used to preheat the tarwater in case B and when the inlet
temperature increases in the tarwater preheater the load decreases in the HEX and less heat is extracted from
the engine cooling water. This effect was not accounted for during the parametric studies but the results
should be unaffected. In the ﬁnal results for the selected working ﬂuid this has been corrected for.

CHAPTER 6
Thermoeconomic analysis
Thermoeconomics combines exergy analysis and engineering economics and sheds light on the price
formation of the exergy streams in a thermal system. It identiﬁes the main cost sources and indicates which
components have the highest potential of being optimized to improve the efﬁciency of the system and lower
the cost of the ﬁnal products produced by the system.
The difference between a conventional economic analysis and a thermoeconomic analysis is ﬁrst and
foremost that much more valuable information can be obtained about the thermal system in a thermoeconomic
analysis. The primary reason for performing a thermoeconomic analysis in this study is to examine the costs
of electricity produced by different components of the plant.
The objective of the following thermoeconomic analysis is to calculate the levelized cost of electricity
for the different power plant conﬁgurations that have been proposed. The purpose is to clarify if higher
efﬁciency of the plant also leads to a lower cost of electricity, i.e. to reveal if the design changes or process
additions can be justiﬁed from an economical viewpoint.
6.1
Thermoeconomic theory
The thermoeconomic analysis in this work is carried out according to the theory described by Bejan
et al. (1996). Using the principles of exergy costing, cost balances are formulated for each component or a
subsystem of components in some occasions. A cost balance for component k is given as
X
e
˙Ce,k + ˙Cw,k = ˙Cq,k +
X
i
˙Ci,k + ˙Zk
(6.1)
A ˙C in equation (6.1) represents a cost rate of an exergy stream in component k. The different exergy
streams considered are material streams into the component (i), material streams out of the component (e),
heat transferred into the component (q) and work done by the component (w). The last term in the equation
covers costs due to capital investment (CI) and operating and maintenance (OM) for component k and can
therefore be expressed as
˙Zk = ˙ZCI
k
+ ˙ZOM
k
(6.2)
This term is elaborated in details in the next two sections. The units of all cost rates are e/h. Equation
(6.1) can also be written as
˙CP,k = ˙CF,k + ˙Zk
(6.3)
85
==PAGE:110==
86
CHAPTER 6. THERMOECONOMIC ANALYSIS
where the P and F subscripts denote product and fuel, respectively. The product of a component
represents the desired result produced by the system while the fuel represents the resources expended to
generate the product.
The cost rates relate to the exergies of the system as follows
˙Ci = ci ˙Ei
(6.4)
˙Ce = ce ˙Ee
(6.5)
˙Cw = cw ˙W
(6.6)
˙Cq = cq ˙Eq
(6.7)
where ci, ce, cw and cq refer to levelized costs per unit of exergy in e/kWh. The exergies are the total
exergies, i.e. the sums of the physical and chemical exergies, and are a direct output of the DNA models.
The dead state pressure and temperature is deﬁned as 1 bar and 25◦C, respectively.
By making cost balances that include all components and tie together the streams of the power plant, a
system of equations is formed that can be solved by deﬁning appropriate auxiliary equations to accompany
each balance. The system of equations was solved in the environment of Engineering equation solver (EES).
6.2
Cost rate of capital investment
The cost rate of capital investment ( ˙ZCI) is the levelized cost of total capital investment (TCI) on an
hourly basis (e/h). The total capital investment is made up of a number of cost elements. These elements
are listed in table 6.1.
Based on the information given in table 6.1 a simpliﬁed relation between TCI and the purchased
equipment cost (PEC) for a new system can be established as (Bejan et al. (1996))
TCI = 6.32PEC
(6.8)
The TCI is levelized on a year basis by multiplication with an annuity factor which is expressed as
f =

trigeneration plant. The TCI can be expressed on an hourly basis by dividing it with the yearly operating
hours of the plant. The resulting parameter is the levelized cost of capital investment and it’s relation to TCI
can be summed up as
˙ZCI = TCI · f

Cost rate of operating and maintenance
A maintenance factor, M, is deﬁned to relate the operating and maintenance costs to the capital investment
cost. As proposed by Lian et al. (2010) the maintenance factor is given a value of M = 1.1 and ˙ZMO and ˙Z
are calculated as follows
˙ZMO = (M −1) ˙ZCI
(6.12)

(6.13)
