6.4
Cost of wood chips
In the following economic analysis the cost of wood chips is a key parameter. The cost in Italy is used
here since the intention is to build the power plant around that location. According to Francescato et al.
(2008) the primary energy cost of wood chips in Italy in 2008 was (VAT excluded)
cwood chips = 0.020 −0.025 e/kWh
This price range is almost identical to what has been predicted as the CIF price of imported biomass in
Denmark in 2015 (Bang et al. (2013)). The higher end of the range will be used in the following analysis.
6.5
Cost of electricity
The cost of electricity produced by the power plant is assumed to be the weighted average of the cost
of power from the gas engine, the steam cycle and, if relevant, the ORC unit. This cost value is the most
important result of the thermoeconomic analysis. It is assumed that all auxiliary equipment running on
electricity receives power from the power plant itself at the calculated price.
Before the cost of electricity is calculated the cost rate of the loss streams from the power plant has to be
taken into account. Heat loss from components is always viewed as exergy destruction rather than exergy
loss. Therefore, only two loss streams are found in the power plant:
• The ﬂue gas from the ﬂue gas fan (node 75 in the DNA models).
• The ﬂue gas condensate wasted from the ﬂue gas condensate splitter (node 76 in the DNA models).
Hence the cost rate of the loss streams is calculated as
˙CL = ˙C75 + ˙C76
(6.14)
The cost rate of the loss streams are added to the cost rates of the power products. The power produced
by the different machines is used as a weighing factor when distributing the cost of the loss streams. As an
example, the ﬁnal cost rate for the engine power is calculated as
˙Ceng = ˙C400 +
Peng
Peng + Psc + Porc
· ˙CL
(6.15)
where the subscript 400 denotes the node number of the electrical power from the engine in the DNA
models. The cost per exergy unit for the engine is then calculated as follows
ceng =
˙Ceng
˙E400
(6.16)
The same method is used to ﬁnd the cost per exergy unit for the ORC and steam cycle power outputs and
the cost of auxiliary power is ﬁnally found by the following
caux = ceng ˙E400 + csc ˙E499 + corc ˙E203
˙E400 + ˙E499 + ˙E203
(6.17)
==PAGE:113==
6.6. COST BALANCES AND PURCHASED EQUIPMENT COSTS
89
where the subscripts 499 and 203 denote the nodes of the electrical power from the steam cycle and ORC
generators, respectively.
It should be mentioned that some components or systems which are a part of the BIGCC are not included
in the thermoeconomic analysis. The components or systems that can be pointed out are:
• The fuel handling module.
• The wet ESP.
• The tar storage tank. This cost is covered with the cost of a counter ﬂow heat exchanger which might
be an underestimation.
• The stack (chimney).
• Water polishing plant for the steam cycle.
along with other systems and devices the author might not be aware of. It is clear though that all the
major and most expensive parts are included in the analysis.
6.6
Cost balances and purchased equipment costs
In subsequent sections, cost balances for the plant components are formulated. Appropriate control
volumes (CV) are deﬁned for each component or group of components and the cost balances are made based
on the exergy streams crossing the CVs. In addition, the PEC for the machinery within each CV is given -
normally as a cost function with reference to the literature. Since the cost estimates all stem from relatively
recently published sources, inﬂation is not accounted for when calculating the PEC.
6.6.1

The control volume of a compressor is shown in ﬁgure 6.1. The product of a compressor is the exergy
difference of the material ﬂowing out and the material ﬂowing in. The fuel is the mechanical work done on
the compressor. The heat lost to the environment contains no exergy. The compressors which are analyzed
include:
• Gas booster
• Steam booster
• Air compressor
• Flue gas fan

appropriate.
6.6.7
Engine
The control volume for the engine is shown in ﬁgure 6.7. The purpose of the component is to produce
electricity but side products are the ﬂue gas and heat which is taken up by the cooling water. It is assumed
that the power produced is the only product of any value. Moreover is the waste heat, which is lost to the
environment, assumed to possess no exergy since it is released at the temperature of the ambient. The fuel is
the dry and cleaned product gas and combustion air.
Figure 6.7: Control volume for the thermoeconomic analysis of the engine.

electrical power cost rate.
The total capital investment cost for an engine was given in Energistyrelsen and Energinet.dk (2012) as
TCIengine = 1250Pengine [e]
(6.39)
The costs of the air coolers that cool the engine cooling water and the cooling loop for the second stage
intercooler are added to the cost of the engine. These costs are found with equation (6.35) from section 6.6.6.

Tarwater throttle

6.6.9
Furnace
The control volume for the furnace is shown in ﬁgure 6.9. The purpose of the furnace is to produce hot
ﬂue gases. In the process some degree of ashes is produced as well. The fuel is the tar from the tar storage
tank, the steam from the same source, the ﬂue gas from the engine and small amount of water that has not
been vaporized.
Figure 6.9: Control volume for the thermoeconomic analysis of the furnace.
The cost balance for the furnace is as follows
c7 ˙E7 + c81 ˙E81 = c55 ˙E55 + c53 ˙E53 + c436 ˙E436 + c423 ˙E423 + ˙Zfurnace
(6.41)

which states that the cost per exergy unit of ash is zero.
According to calculations made by Vølund the purchase cost of the furnace is around
PECfurnace = 500000 [$]
(6.43)
6.6.10
Flue gas cooler
The control volume for the ﬂue gas cooler is shown in ﬁgure 6.10. The purpuse of the ﬂue gas cooler is
to cool the ﬂue gas and condense some of the water from it. It is water cooled and therefore analyzed as a
regular counter ﬂow heat exchanger.

96

c73 ˙E73 + c74 ˙E74 −c731 ˙E731 = c601 ˙E601 −c602 ˙E602 + ˙Zflue gas cooler

c73 = c74

(6.45)
It is assumed that the condensate and the cool ﬂue gas share the same cost per exergy unit. The cooling
water has a constant cost per exergy unit of zero.
The cost of the ﬂue gas cooler is calculated by means of equation (6.25) from section 6.6.3.
6.6.11
Heat recovery steam generator
The control volume for the heat recovery steam generator (HRSG) is shown in ﬁgure 6.11. The HRSG
produces superheated steam for the turbine and a small amount of saturated steam for the deaerator. The
heat is extracted from the ﬂue gas from the furnace.
Figure 6.11: Control volume for the thermoeconomic analysis of the Heat recovery steam generator.
The cost balance for the HRSG is given as
c54 ˙E54 + c514 ˙E514 −c50 ˙E50 = c7 ˙E7 −c72 ˙E72 + ˙Zhrsg

The ﬂue gas has a constant cost per exergy unit of throughout the heat exchange.

6.6.13
Generator
The control volume of a generator is shown in ﬁgure 6.13. It converts mechanical power to electrical
power and looses some amount of heat to the ambient. The waste heat has no exergy since it is assumed to
be released at the temperature of the ambient.
Figure 6.13: Control volume for the thermoeconomic analysis of the generator.
The cost balance of a generator is given as
ce,499 ˙Ee,499 =w,401 ˙Ew,401 + ˙Zgenerator
(6.59)
No auxiliary relations are needed for the generator.

PECgenerator = 60P 0.5
generator [$]

where the electrical power is in kW.
6.6.14
Steam condenser
A control volume for a condenser is shown in ﬁgure 6.14. The functionality of the condenser is to
condense the steam from the turbine to a saturated liquid. Air is used as a cooling media and the heat that is
lost from the condenser contains no exergy since it is assumed to be released at the ambient temperature.
Figure 6.14: Control volume for the thermoeconomic analysis of the air cooled condenser.
The cost balance for the condenser is formulated as follows
==PAGE:123==
6.6. COST BALANCES AND PURCHASED EQUIPMENT COSTS
99
c56 ˙E56 −c541 ˙E541 = ˙Zcondenser
(6.61)
No auxiliary relations are needed.
The purchase cost of a steam condenser is assumed to be the same as for an air cooler. Equation (6.35)
is used to calculate the cost and the same method is used as described in section 6.6.6. A heat transfer
coefﬁcient of 0.75 kW/(m2K) is assumed to be appropriate for the steam condenser.
6.6.15
Deaerator
The control volume for the deaerator is shown in ﬁgure 6.15. It is treated like a mixer where the product
is saturated water and the fuel is the saturated steam from the HRSG and the condensate from the LP pump.
Figure 6.15: Control volume for the thermoeconomic analysis of the deaerator.
The cost balance is given as follows
c59 ˙E59 = c514 ˙E514 + c58 ˙E58 + ˙Zdeaerator
(6.62)
No auxiliary relations are needed.

PECdeaerator = 6014 ˙m0.7
steam [$]
(6.63)
where the mass ﬂow is in kg/s and refers to the mass ﬂow at the outlet.
6.6.16
Humidiﬁer
The control volume for the humidiﬁer is shown in ﬁgure 6.16. The purpose of the component is to heat
up the air and humidify it up to the saturation limit. The control volume contains a number of components
from the model but since they constitute only one component (a humidiﬁer) in reality, it is regarded sensible
to analyze them in this combined manner.
The cost balance for the humidiﬁer is given as follows
c804 ˙E804 −c822 ˙E822 −c828 ˙E828 = c66 ˙E66 −c67 ˙E67 + ˙Zhumidifier
(6.64)
and one auxiliary equation is needed
c66 = c67 = 0
(6.65)
which states that the cost per exergy unit for the hot ﬂuid is constant.
The purchase cost of the humidiﬁer is assumed to be close to the cost of a heat exchanger and hence
calculated with equation (6.25) from section 6.6.3.

6.6.17
ORC
The entire ORC unit is analyzed as one component in the thermoeconomic analysis. The control volume
is shown in ﬁgure 6.17. The product of the ORC is the electricity produced in the generator. The fuel is
the pump power and the exergy difference on the inlets and outlets on the hot ﬂuid sides of the economizer
and the evaporator. The waste heat from the condenser and the generator are assumed to be released at the
ambient temperature and hence contain no exergy.
Figure 6.17: Control volume for the thermoeconomic analysis of the ORC.
The cost balance of the ORC unit is given as follows
c203 ˙E203 = ceco,in ˙Eeco,in−ceco,out ˙Eeco,out
+
X
i
 ceva,in ˙Eeva,in −ceva,out ˙Eeva,out

i + caux ˙E202 + ˙Zorc
(6.66)
where i = 1, 2, ..., n and n denotes the number evaporators connected in parallel. Two auxiliary
equations are needed
ceco,out = ceco,in
ceva,out,i = ceva,in,i
(6.67)
==PAGE:125==
6.7. CONVERTING DOLLARS TO EUROS
101
which state that the cost per exergy unit of the hot ﬂuids in the HEXs are constant through the processes.
The cost of the ORC unit is computed by adding the costs of each component in the cycle which are
found in the following way:
• The cost of the heat exchangers is calculated with the procedure from section 6.6.3.
• The cost of the generator is found by means of equation (6.60) in section 6.6.13.
• The cost of the pump is found by equation (6.21) in section 6.6.2.
• The cost of the air cooled condenser is calculated with equation (6.35) from section 6.6.6. For this to
be possible the overall heat transfer coefﬁcient has to be estimated. In Astolﬁet al. (2014) a correlation
is used for this purpose. The method is adopted here and is given with the following equations
k = −9.84δ2 + 3.61δ + 0.972 [kW/(m2K)]
δ = Th,out −Tc,out
Th,out −Tc,in
(6.68)
When using an air inlet temperature of 25◦C and outlet temperature of 30◦C, this procedure gives a
value of k = 0.317 kW/(m2K) which falls in the range found in tables (e.g. in Holman (2010)) for the
condensation of an organic ﬂuid in an air cooler.
• The cost of the turbine is found with equation (6.58) from section 6.6.12.
6.7
Converting dollars to euros
Dollars are converted to euros by using a conversion factor of 0.88.
6.8
Level of accuracy of the cost analysis
When using the cost functions for the components it is often recommended to use correction factors such
as a pressure factor, correction factor due to use of special materials etc. This is not done here except in the
case of the HRSG. This might cause the cost estimates to be somewhat inaccurate in some instances but
should still give fairly good results that can be used for the comparison of the different design cases.
==PAGE:126==
102
CHAPTER 6. THERMOECONOMIC ANALYSIS
6.9
Results of the thermoeconomic analysis
The main results of the thermoeconomic analysis are:
• The PECs and cost rates ( ˙Zk) of all components and the whole power plant.
• The cost rates of the power outputs, auxiliary power, loss streams and the fuel.
• The levelized cost of electricity for the whole power plant and for the different power outputs.
As an example of the thermoeconomic analysis the EES codes and full node results are given for cases
A, C2 and DA1 in appendix F.
The component purchase costs (PECs) for all components can be seen in table 6.3 and the cost rates in
table 6.4. There is no PEC given for the engine since the TCI was calculated directly with equation (6.39).
The TCI for other components can easily be found from the values in table 6.3 by multiplying with 6.32
according to equation (6.8).

From the two tables it is obvious that the gasiﬁer is by far the most expensive component. The second
most expensive component is the engine. It has a cost of around 1/10 of the cost of the gasiﬁer and does not
vary much between design cases. The furnace cost is constant for all cases and is around 1/20 of the cost of
the gasiﬁer.
The cost of most of the other components varies considerably between design cases. In that respect the
HRSG and the steam turbine have quite large cost ﬂuctuations as well as the ORC of course. The HEXs
which are a part of the HEN for each case (as seen in the HEN ﬁgures in chapters 4 and 5) are also subject to
cost changes between the different cases.
The total cost of the steam cycle, HEN and ORC is compared for the seven design cases in ﬁgure 6.18. It
can be seen how the steam cycle cost increases from case A through cases B and C as the pinch point in the
HRSG is lowered and the power production goes up. In case C2 the cost of the steam cycle still increases
mainly due to a higher cost of the HRSG and to a lesser extent of the turbine. The HEN referred to in the
ﬁgure covers all heat exchangers in the power plant except for the HRSG and the condensers in the steam
cycle and ORC. The cost of the HEN is lowered from case A through cases B and C. This is mainly because
of less use of gas-liquid or gas-gas HEXs and enhanced use of liquid-liquid HEXs instead. As the ORC is
added the cost of the HEN rises again and continuous to do so as the ORC HEXs grow larger. These HEXs
==PAGE:128==
104
CHAPTER 6. THERMOECONOMIC ANALYSIS
are expensive because they have a low pinch point temperature difference and often gas on one side. The
ORC cost rises from case DA1 through cases DA2 and DB as a consequence of the increased HEX costs.

a dominating cost factor for case DB.

The cost rates of produced power, auxiliary power, loss streams and wood are shown in table 6.7. The
cost rate of wood is of course constant. The cost rate of loss streams is also fairly constant except for case
DB which can be explained by more cooling of the ﬂue gas in the ORC ﬂue gas evaporator for that case.
More cost is taken up by the ORC and less by the loss stream.
The cost of the loss stream has been distributed to the cost rates of produced power. The case with the
highest cost rate is case DB and the one with the lowest one is case A. the cost rate of auxiliary power
increases notably for cases B, C and C2 mainly due to the increased power consumption of the steam booster.

The most important results of the thermoeconomic analysis are shown in table 6.8. The levelized cost of
electricity (LCOE) can be used to compare the design cases and estimate if the proposed design changes are
cost-effective. According to the results given in the table the most cost-effective design is case C2 and the
least cost effective design is case DA2. The design changes made in cases B and C lead to similar LCOEs
and it is clear that those changes represent non-negligible improvements of the design. The ORC integration
in case DA1 leads to a lower LCOE than in case A but still much higher than in cases B, C and C2. The
ORC integration in cases DA2 and DB is not cost-effective since the LCOE is higher in both those cases
than in the cases that the ORC was integrated with (A and B).
The LCOE of the power produced by the engine, steam cycle and ORC are also displayed in the table.
The design modiﬁcations lead to changes in these values.

The results obtained from the thermoeconomic analysis rely heavily on the economic parameters deﬁned
in sections 6.2 and 6.3. The main purpose of the analysis is nevertheless to enable a comparison of different
design options. The conclusions made based on this comparison are unaffected by these parameters and also
by the cost of wood chips because the results for all design cases would have been affected in the same way.

improved efﬁciency and lower LCOE. Cases B, C and C2 are considered to be cost-effective in the table
because their LCOE is lower than the basic design, which is case A. The efﬁciency in case DA1 is higher
than in case A and the LCOE is lower. The design change in case DA1 is regarded cost-effective but not in
cases DA2 and DB.

==PAGE:133==
CHAPTER 8
Conclusions
A biomass integrated gasiﬁcation combined cycle (BIGCC) is modeled based on a concept design
developed by Babcock & Wilcox Vølund. The basic model relies on target data retrieved from the company.
The model is for a power plant with 4 MW net power output and an electrical efﬁciency of 36.2%. The
model resembles the target data reasonably well and reveals that the waste heat of the power plant cannot
cover the internal heat needs.
The plant is scaled up to have a net power output of 5 MW in design case A. When choosing the design
point an optimum is found that maximizes the heat recovery in the steam cycle and ensures that all heat
needs can be covered with waste heat from the plant. The electrical efﬁciency is only 35.68% and further
improvements are sought.
The largest heat need in the power plant is the tarwater evaporation. In cases B and C the pressure in
the tarwater storage tank is lowered in order to be able to use the engine cooling water to evaporate the
tarwater instead of the ﬂue gas. This allows more heat to be extracted in the heat recovery steam generator.
In case B the optimum storage tank pressure is 0.32 bar and the electrical efﬁciency is 37.41%. In case C the
optimum storage tank pressure is 0.27 bar and the electrical efﬁciency is 37.88%. The higher efﬁciency in
case C comes from the fact that the pinch point temperature difference in the heat recovery steam generator
is 62.3◦C in case B but only 10◦C in case C. In both cases the heat needs can be covered by the heat sources.
In case C2 the live steam pressure and temperature are raised in the steam cycle. The efﬁciency increases
substantially and this case has the highest efﬁciency of all design cases: 39.15%.
An organic Rankine cycle (ORC) is integrated with case A in cases DA1 and DA2. More power (137
kW) is produced when the heat sources are utilized in two evaporators and a preheater (case DA2) and less
is produced (125.2 kW) when only one evaporator is employed (case DA1). The evaporating temperature is
nevertheless higher in case DA1 resulting in a higher cycle efﬁciency (6.55%) than in case DA2 (5.68%).
The optimum working ﬂuid for both cases is R1234yf.
Integrating an ORC with case B in case DB only results in 86.3 kW power output and an efﬁciency of
3.43%. The optimum working ﬂuid is R1234yf.
A thermoeconomic analysis is performed to calculate the levelized cost of electricity (LCOE) for each
design case. The main results are that other measures should be taken to make better use of waste heat
streams in the power plant than integrating an ORC. The temperatures of the heat sources are too low and a
cost-effective design can not be achieved. Although case DA1 results in a lower LCOE than case A, other
options such as cases B, C or C2, have even lower LCOE.
Lowering the pressure in the tar storage tank has extremely positive effects on the power plant. It allows
more heat to be utilized in the steam cycle and by raising the live steam pressure and temperature as well the
efﬁciency is raised from 35.68% to 39.15%. This design change also lowers the LCOE from 0.3452 e/kWh
109
==PAGE:134==
110
CHAPTER 8. CONCLUSIONS
to 0.3247 e/kWh which shows that this modiﬁcation is indeed cost-effective as well.
Further work on the BIGCC could include making the gasiﬁer component in DNA even more ﬂexible and
better suited for off-design analysis. An off-design analysis would be an interesting task since the operation
of the plant is subject to considerable ﬂuctuations in product gas composition and amount of tars in the gas.
A thermoeconomic optimization might also reveal some good design options.
